###begin article-title 0
###xml 101 106 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
Csm4, in Collaboration with Ndj1, Mediates Telomere-Led Chromosome Dynamics and Recombination during Yeast Meiosis
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
Conceived and designed the experiments: JJW KPK RK SZ BW NK EA. Performed the experiments: JJW KPK RK SZ BW. Analyzed the data: JJW KPK RK SZ BW NK EA. Wrote the paper: JJW KPK RK SZ NK EA.
###end p 1
###begin article-title 2
Chromosome Mechanics and Meiotic Engine Maintenance
###end article-title 2
###begin article-title 3
Csm4-Dependent Telomere Movement on Nuclear Envelope Promotes Meiotic Recombination
###end article-title 3
###begin p 4
###xml 82 87 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 398 409 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">participant</span>
Chromosome movements are a general feature of mid-prophase of meiosis. In budding yeast, meiotic chromosomes exhibit dynamic movements, led by nuclear envelope (NE)-associated telomeres, throughout the zygotene and pachytene stages. Zygotene motion underlies the global tendency for colocalization of NE-associated chromosome ends in a "bouquet." In this study, we identify Csm4 as a new molecular participant in these processes and show that, unlike the two previously identified components, Ndj1 and Mps3, Csm4 is not required for meiosis-specific telomere/NE association. Instead, it acts to couple telomere/NE ensembles to a force generation mechanism. Mutants lacking Csm4 and/or Ndj1 display the following closely related phenotypes: (i) elevated crossover (CO) frequencies and decreased CO interference without abrogation of normal pathways; (ii) delayed progression of recombination, and recombination-coupled chromosome morphogenesis, with resulting delays in the MI division; and (iii) nondisjunction of homologs at the MI division for some reason other than absence of (the obligatory) CO(s). The recombination effects are discussed in the context of a model where the underlying defect is chromosome movement, the absence of which results in persistence of inappropriate chromosome relationships that, in turn, results in the observed mutant phenotypes.
###end p 4
###begin title 5
Author Summary
###end title 5
###begin p 6
###xml 480 486 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">humans</span>
###xml 822 827 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
In meiosis, cells specified to become gametes (eggs or sperm) undergo a single round of DNA replication followed by two consecutive chromosomal divisions. In most organisms, the proper segregation of chromosomes at the first meiotic division is mechanically dependent upon genetic exchange, or crossing over, at homologous sites along chromosomes. This process is highly regulated so that every pair of matched chromosomes, regardless of size, receives at least one crossover. In humans, defects in this recombination process can lead to a variety of chromosome aneuploidy syndromes. During early stages in meiosis, the ends of chromosomes, called telomeres, associate with the envelope of the nucleus and undergo highly dynamic movements. We identified a new component of the movement-generating system, Csm4, in budding yeast. In the absence of Csm4, the telomeres associate with the nuclear envelope but are locked in an immobile state. In addition, strains lacking Csm4 show delayed recombination progression and high levels of chromosome mis-segregation at the first meiotic division. These findings suggest that, during meiosis, Csm4 is involved in coupling telomere complexes to the movement-generating system and that chromosome motion is important for the completion of early steps in recombination.
###end p 6
###begin title 7
Introduction
###end title 7
###begin p 8
###xml 423 426 423 426 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Zickler1">[1]</xref>
###xml 428 431 428 431 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Scherthan1">[2]</xref>
###xml 432 435 432 435 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Tomita1">[4]</xref>
###xml 633 636 633 636 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Scherthan1">[2]</xref>
###xml 637 640 637 640 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken1">[5]</xref>
###xml 641 644 641 644 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Koszul1">[6]</xref>
###xml 915 918 915 918 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Koszul1">[6]</xref>
###xml 1151 1154 1151 1154 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Miki1">[7]</xref>
###xml 449 454 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 967 980 <span type="species:ncbi:4896">fission yeast</span>
Classical cytological studies have shown that during the zygotene stage of meiosis, chromosome ends are tightly and specifically associated with the nuclear envelope (NE) and move coordinately into a "bouquet" configuration such that they are localized within a sub-area of the nuclear periphery. Upon exit from this stage, during early pachytene, telomeres again redistribute throughout the nuclear periphery (reviewed in [1]; [2]-[4]). In budding yeast, these global effects are achieved by means of highly dynamic, actin-dependent, telomere-led movements, which, after initiating at the onset of zygotene, continue into pachytene [2],[5],[6]. Recent work from one of our laboratories shows that telomeres and associated nuclear envelope (NE) segments move via passive association with nucleus-hugging segments of dynamic cytoskeletal actin cables that tend to form in the vicinity of the spindle pole body (SPB; [6]). A different mechanism has been elucidated for fission yeast; telomeres are tightly and specifically associated with the SPB and the entire complex moves dynamically along microtubules via interaction with the dynein motor complex [7].
###end p 8
###begin p 9
###xml 75 78 75 78 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Schmitt1">[8]</xref>
###xml 317 325 317 325 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. pombe</italic>
###xml 573 576 573 576 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Chikashige1">[3]</xref>
###xml 577 580 577 580 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Davis1">[9]</xref>
###xml 667 671 667 671 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Miki2">[10]</xref>
###xml 791 794 791 794 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Chikashige1">[3]</xref>
###xml 795 798 795 798 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Miki1">[7]</xref>
###xml 920 924 920 924 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Chua1">[11]</xref>
###xml 925 929 925 929 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad1">[12]</xref>
###xml 940 944 940 944 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad2">[13]</xref>
###xml 1130 1133 1130 1133 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Scherthan1">[2]</xref>
###xml 1134 1138 1134 1138 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Chua1">[11]</xref>
###xml 1139 1143 1139 1143 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad1">[12]</xref>
###xml 1144 1148 1144 1148 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Rockmill1">[14]</xref>
###xml 1149 1153 1149 1153 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken2">[15]</xref>
###xml 1253 1257 1253 1257 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad2">[13]</xref>
###xml 1876 1879 1876 1879 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Koszul1">[6]</xref>
###xml 11 24 <span type="species:ncbi:4896">fission yeast</span>
###xml 34 39 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 60 63 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 69 74 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 317 325 <span type="species:ncbi:4896">S. pombe</span>
###xml 811 816 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 1587 1592 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Studies in fission yeast, budding yeast (references below), rat, and mouse [8] have shown that, in accord with their special functions, telomeres of meiotic chromosomes become robustly associated with the NE in complexes comprised of both meiosis-specific proteins and proteins recruited from the mitotic program. In S. pombe meiosis, Bqt1 and Bqt2 connect the telomere binding protein Rap1, which associates with telomeres through interactions with Taz1, to the spindle pole body protein Sad1. Sad1 is a member of the SUN domain family of proteins that localize to the NE [3],[9]. Sad1 is also known to interact with the spindle pole body binding (SPB) protein Kms1 [10]. The final telomere/SPB cluster is thought to form through interactions between Bqt1, Bqt2, Rap1, Taz1, Sad1, and Kms1 [3],[7]. In budding yeast, two components of meiotic telomere-NE ensembles have been identified thus far: Ndj1, also called Tam1 [11],[12], and Mps3 [13]. Ndj1 is a meiosis-specific protein that mediates association of telomeres to the NE; as a result, in the absence of Ndj1, global and dynamic chromosome movements are severely reduced ([2],[11],[12],[14],[15]; this work). Mps3, which is present in mitotic as well as meiotic cells, most likely has two roles [13]. First, it interacts directly with Ndj1 such that the two proteins display a mutually dependent requirement for telomere localization to the NE. Second, Mps3 is a SUN domain protein, which suggests that it may mediate interactions between telomeres and cytoskeletal determinants. Recent studies have shown that rapid movement of yeast pachytene chromosomes involves passive association of telomere/NE ensembles to dynamically moving actin cables. Within this mechanically integrated complex, force is exerted on the NE component and transduced via telomere/NE complexes through the NE to the associated chromosome end [6].
###end p 9
###begin p 10
###xml 293 306 293 306 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 308 317 308 313 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 726 730 722 726 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Chua1">[11]</xref>
###xml 732 736 728 732 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad1">[12]</xref>
###xml 738 742 734 738 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Wu1">[16]</xref>
###xml 743 747 739 743 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Goldman1">[18]</xref>
###xml 752 760 748 756 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">S. pombe</italic>
###xml 925 928 921 924 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Chikashige1">[3]</xref>
###xml 929 933 925 929 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Yamamoto1">[19]</xref>
###xml 1520 1523 1516 1519 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Zickler1">[1]</xref>
###xml 1524 1527 1520 1523 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Koszul1">[6]</xref>
###xml 1528 1532 1524 1528 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Rasmussen1">[20]</xref>
###xml 1533 1537 1529 1533 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Scherthan2">[21]</xref>
###xml 133 146 <span type="species:ncbi:4896">fission yeast</span>
###xml 159 164 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 293 306 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 752 760 <span type="species:ncbi:4896">S. pombe</span>
###xml 874 879 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
The functional role(s) of global and dynamic chromosome movements for meiosis, though widely discussed, are not established. In both fission yeast and budding yeast, situations in which telomere localization is aberrant or the motion mechanism is directly abrogated reveal diverse defects. In S. cerevisiae, ndj1Delta strains show levels of crossing over similar to wild-type (WT), partially disrupted crossover (CO) interference, modestly increased ectopic recombination, delayed formation of tight juxtaposition of homologs including delayed formation of the synaptonemal complex (SC), defective progression of recombination intermediates into mature recombinants, increased MI nondisjunction, and decreased spore viability [11], [12], [16]-[18]. In S. pombe, similar phenotypes are observed for mutants defective in telomere localization; however, in contrast to budding yeast, CO-levels are significantly decreased (e.g. [3],[19]). Furthermore, in this organism, meiosis does not involve CO interference or the SC, and it has been difficult to perform a detailed analysis of recombination intermediates and their timing. These findings have led to suggestions that motion might play a direct role in recombination and/or homolog juxtaposition. However, the pleiotropic nature of these effects have made it difficult to distinguish defects that are direct consequences of the absence of motion rather than indirect effects and/or those that result from aberrant telomere biology irrespective of motion. We and others [1],[6],[20],[21] have argued that the primary role of movement is to eliminate aberrant topological relationships among chromosomes, e.g. entanglements or "interlocks" and/or other types of unprogrammed connections among nonhomologous chromosomes, a possibility that has not yet been directly assessed in any organism.
###end p 10
###begin p 11
###xml 154 163 154 159 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 309 313 305 309 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Rabitsch1">[22]</xref>
###xml 497 501 493 497 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">CSM4</italic>
###xml 580 584 576 580 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">NDJ1</italic>
###xml 823 832 819 824 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 837 846 829 834 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 1319 1323 1307 1311 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Kosaka1">[23]</xref>
###xml 1334 1338 1322 1326 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad3">[24]</xref>
###xml 638 649 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">participant</span>
The present study began with a search for mutations that affect recombination through telomere-dependent effects. The hallmark phenotype conferred by the ndj1Delta mutation is increased nondisjunction of homologs at the MI division, and a screen for mutants defective in chromosome segregation during meiosis [22] identified three additional genes with weak chromosome missegregation phenotypes. We began by further characterizing these missegregation phenotypes. We show that one of these genes, CSM4, is required specifically for regular segregation of homologs, analogously to NDJ1, and by several additional criteria, encodes a third participant in the meiotic telomere/NE interactions involved in motion. We further show that the role of Csm4 is distinct from that of either Ndj1 or Mps3. Finally, we analyzed diverse csm4Delta and ndj1Delta phenotypes for motion at zygotene and pachytene, recombination (by genetic and physical approaches), SC morphogenesis, and meiotic progression. The observed phenotypes suggest a role for chromosome motion that can explain all observed effects and also supports the idea that the primary role of motion is regularization of topological relationships among chromosomes. Related and complementary findings are presented in the accompanying paper by Shinohara and colleagues ([23], see also [24]).
###end p 11
###begin title 12
Results
###end title 12
###begin title 13
Analysis of Chromosome Mis-Segregation
###end title 13
###begin p 14
###xml 90 94 90 94 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Chua1">[11]</xref>
###xml 95 97 95 97 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad1">12</xref>
###xml 125 134 125 130 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 138 139 134 135 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">n</underline>
###xml 141 142 137 138 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">d</underline>
###xml 144 145 140 141 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">j</underline>
###xml 292 296 288 292 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Chua1">[11]</xref>
###xml 297 301 293 297 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad1">[12]</xref>
###xml 436 440 432 436 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">CSM2</italic>
###xml 442 446 438 442 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">CSM3</italic>
###xml 452 456 448 452 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">CSM4</italic>
###xml 458 459 454 455 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">c</underline>
###xml 469 470 465 466 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">s</underline>
###xml 484 485 480 481 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">m</underline>
###xml 493 497 489 493 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Rabitsch1">[22]</xref>
###xml 568 577 564 569 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 62 67 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Ndj1 (also called Tam1) was the first identified component of yeast telomere/NE ensembles [11],12. The hallmark phenotype of ndj1Delta is nondisjunction of homologs at the first meiotic division, with an accompanying modest decrease in spore viability, to 62-82%, as compared to 92-98% in WT [11],[12]. To identify new mutations that affect chromosomal events during meiosis, and in particular recombination, we focused on three genes, CSM2, CSM3, and CSM4 (chromosome segregation in meiosis; [22]), whose corresponding mutations confer phenotypes similar to those of ndj1Delta: decreased spore viability and aberrant meiotic chromosome segregation.
###end p 14
###begin title 15
###xml 36 45 36 41 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
MI Homolog Nondisjunction Occurs in csm4Delta Mutants
###end title 15
###begin p 16
###xml 90 93 90 93 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm</italic>
###xml 142 151 142 147 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 309 313 305 309 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-RossMacdonald1">[25]</xref>
###xml 328 331 324 327 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm</italic>
###xml 346 355 342 347 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 379 388 371 380 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g001">Figure 1A</xref>
###xml 655 659 647 651 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Beilharz1">[26]</xref>
We began by further characterizing the nature of the chromosome mis-segregation defect in csm mutants. Spore viability patterns revealed that csm4Delta confers a pattern that is diagnostic of homolog nondisjunction: an excess of tetrads containing 0, 2, or 4 viable spores as compared to 1 or 3 viable spores [25]. Of the three csm mutants, only csm4Delta displays this pattern (Figure 1A, data not shown). Csm4 was identified by bioinformatic analysis as a 156 amino acid tail-anchored membrane protein. Consistent with this designation, Csm4 was observed in both the endoplasmic reticulum and the perinuclear membrane when overproduced in mitotic cells [26].
###end p 16
###begin title 17
Distribution of viable spores in tetrads dissected from the indicated EAY1108/EAY1112 derived strains.
###end title 17
###begin p 18
###xml 392 401 392 397 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 403 412 399 404 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 418 437 410 421 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916; csm4&#916;</italic>
A) In all plots the horizontal axes correspond to the classes of tetrads with 4, 3, 2, 1, and 0 viable spores, and the vertical axes correspond to the percentage of each class. The total number of tetrads dissected (n) and the overall spore viability (SV) are shown for each genotype. B) Histograms representing percent total spore viability and the proportion of four viable tetrads for WT, csm4Delta, ndj1Delta, and ndj1Delta csm4Delta are presented.
###end p 18
###begin p 19
###xml 40 49 40 45 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
The homolog nondisjunction phenotype of csm4Delta was confirmed and extended as follows:
###end p 19
###begin p 20
###xml 150 154 150 154 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 159 163 159 163 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">TRP1</italic>
###xml 165 169 165 169 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Argueso1">[27]</xref>
###xml 171 179 171 179 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pgen.1000188.s005">Table S1</xref>
###xml 458 462 458 462 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Argueso1">[27]</xref>
###xml 463 467 463 467 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Khazanehdari1">[28]</xref>
###xml 524 533 524 529 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
- One approach utilized congenic SK1 strains EAY1108/EAY1112 in which one chromosome (XV; 1040 kb) is heterozygous for the centromere-linked markers, URA3 and TRP1 ([27], Table S1). In this background, analysis of asci containing two viable spores can distinguish a MI segregation defect from random spore death. In the former case, the two spores often contain the same (sister) centromeric marker while, in the latter case, there is no such tendency (e.g. [27],[28]). Consistent with an MI defect, 88% of two-spore viable csm4Delta tetrads (n = 212) contained sister centromere markers.
###end p 20
###begin p 21
###xml 246 247 246 247 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 251 252 251 252 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">+</sup>
###xml 257 266 257 262 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 489 498 485 490 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mlh1&#916;</italic>
###xml 519 528 511 516 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">msh5&#916;</italic>
- In the absence of any other defect, nondisjunction of the centromere-marked chromosome XV homologs will result in two-spore viable tetrads in which each spore contains one centromere from each homolog; as a result, both spore clones will be Ura+ Trp+. In csm4Delta 1.4 % of the two-spore viable tetrads were of this type (n = 212). This level of homolog nondisjunction is very similar to that observed in the analysis of two recombination mutants known to confer homolog nondisjunction: mlh1Delta (1.2%, n = 324) and msh5Delta (3.4%, n = 994).
###end p 21
###begin p 22
###xml 169 172 169 172 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MAT</italic>
###xml 178 186 178 182 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">MAT&#945;</italic>
###xml 297 306 293 298 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 376 385 368 373 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">msh5&#916;</italic>
###xml 149 154 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
- Analogously, nondisjunction of the unmarked chromosome III of these strains will yield two-spore viable tetrads in which the two spores carry both yeast mating types (MATa and MATalpha). Such spores can be detected because they fail to mate with haploid tester strains of either mating type. In csm4Delta, 7.8% (n = 103) were of this type, with a similar level observed for msh5Delta (7.1%, n = 56).
###end p 22
###begin p 23
###xml 139 148 139 144 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 222 226 218 222 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Rockmill2">[29]</xref>
###xml 303 312 299 304 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 363 371 355 363 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pgen.1000188.s005">Table S1</xref>
###xml 373 382 365 370 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 510 519 498 503 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
- In contrast, another common type of MI missegregation, precocious segregation of sister chromatids (PSSC), occurs at a very low level in csm4Delta. PSSC can be identified by analyzing two- and three-spore viable tetrads [29]. In the EAY1108/EAY1112 strain set, PSSC was not detectable in either WT or csm4Delta; in another background (SK1 isogenic NH942/NH943; Table S1) csm4Delta strains showed 5 PSSC events in 1284 tetrads (0.39%) and WT showed no PSSC events in 646 WT tetrads (difference between WT and csm4Delta values is not statistically significant, Fisher's Exact Test).
###end p 23
###begin p 24
###xml 75 84 75 80 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 166 170 162 166 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Kosaka1">[23]</xref>
Taken together, these analyses show that the primary segregation defect in csm4Delta mutants is homolog nondisjunction. Similar results are reported by Kosaka et al. [23].
###end p 24
###begin title 25
Csm4 and Ndj1 Play Functionally Related Roles for Homolog Disjunction
###end title 25
###begin p 26
###xml 61 70 61 66 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 110 119 106 111 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 121 130 113 122 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g001">Figure 1A</xref>
###xml 158 167 150 155 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 300 309 288 293 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 314 323 298 303 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 325 334 305 314 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g001">Figure 1B</xref>
###xml 409 418 389 394 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 437 446 413 418 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 546 555 518 523 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 578 586 546 554 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g001">Figure 1</xref>
###xml 700 715 668 683 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g001">Figure 1A and B</xref>
###xml 901 907 869 875 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">weaker</italic>
###xml 955 963 923 931 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">stronger</italic>
###xml 1072 1081 1040 1045 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 1140 1149 1104 1109 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 1383 1392 1343 1348 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 1394 1403 1350 1355 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 1409 1428 1361 1372 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916; ndj1&#916;</italic>
Comparison of isogenic strains reveals that the phenotype of csm4Delta is significantly stronger than that of ndj1Delta (Figure 1A). As previously mentioned, csm4Delta mutants display a spore viability pattern indicative of nondisjunction (4, 2, 0>3, 1 viable spores). This pattern is more severe in csm4Delta vs. ndj1Delta (Figure 1B). Further, a larger percentage of two-spore viable tetrads are sisters in csm4Delta (88%) compared to ndj1Delta (69%). As judged by the first two of these phenotypes, the double mutant defect is very similar to ndj1Delta, but slightly weaker (Figure 1). These same patterns are also apparent in the percentage of four viable spore asci and overall spore viability (Figure 1A and B). These mutant phenotypes imply functional interaction between Csm4 and Ndj1 with respect to MI homolog disjunction. The observed epistatic relationship is intriguing. First, it is the weaker phenotype that dominates (is epistatic to) the stronger phenotype. Second, the occurrence of slight synergy implies that not only is Ndj1 strongly required for the csm4Delta phenotype but conversely, Csm4 is weakly required for the ndj1Delta phenotype. Importantly, the above conclusions do not reflect differences in sporulation efficiencies. For WT, 82% of cells yielded asci with three or four spores and 89% yielded asci with at least one spore (n = 234 cells examined); csm4Delta, ndj1Delta, and csm4Delta ndj1Delta all exhibited similar reductions in both categories: 50, 51, and 64% respectively, and 73, 59, and 78% respectively (n = 229, 221, and 238 cells examined, respectively).
###end p 26
###begin title 27
Telomere/NE Analysis
###end title 27
###begin p 28
###xml 150 158 150 158 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pgen.1000188.s005">Table S1</xref>
###xml 299 308 299 308 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2A</xref>
###xml 319 322 319 322 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken1">[5]</xref>
###xml 774 783 774 783 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2B</xref>
###xml 794 803 794 803 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pgen.1000188.s001">Figure S1</xref>
###xml 805 813 805 813 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pgen.1000188.s005">Table S1</xref>
###xml 815 836 815 836 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Materials and Methods</xref>
Association of telomeres with the NE, occurrence of the bouquet configuration, and dynamic telomere movements were assessed, in SK1 isogenic strains (Table S1) by analyzing the disposition of Rap1-GFP foci. Rap1 localizes prominently and focally at telomeres and less markedly throughout chromatin (Figure 2A, panel i; [5]). Our approach can detect foci that correspond to single bivalent telomeres at pachytene (R. Koszul, unpublished data), and thus, for earlier stages, should be sensitive enough to detect clusters of two (or more) unpaired homolog telomeres or four (or more) individual chromatids. Since bouquet formation involves colocalization of telomeres near, but not at, the SPB (Introduction), we used strains in which the SPB was also labeled, with Spc42-RFP (Figure 2B, panel i; Figure S1; Table S1; Materials and Methods).
###end p 28
###begin title 29
###xml 42 51 42 47 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 57 66 53 58 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
Telomere organization and dynamics in WT, ndj1Delta, and csm4Delta mutants.
###end title 29
###begin p 30
###xml 370 391 370 391 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Materials and Methods</xref>
###xml 471 480 471 476 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 482 492 478 484 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;,</italic>
###xml 497 516 489 500 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916; csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 1365 1386 1349 1370 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Materials and Methods</xref>
###xml 1853 1862 1837 1846 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pgen.1000188.s001">Figure S1</xref>
###xml 1942 1951 1926 1931 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 1953 1963 1933 1939 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;,</italic>
###xml 1968 1987 1944 1955 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916; csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 2308 2327 2264 2275 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;/csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 2409 2418 2357 2362 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 2420 2429 2364 2369 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 2435 2454 2375 2386 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916; csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 2482 2503 2414 2435 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Materials and Methods</xref>
###xml 3018 3038 2948 2968 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Material and Methods</xref>
A) Telomere organization in strains whose telomeres are illuminated by Rap1-GFP. i Telomere organization in fixed prophase nuclei. Nuclei were distributed into two classes, "peripheral" (top panel; 10 frames from top to bottom) and "dispersed" (bottom panel) depending on the absence or presence of distinguishable Rap1-GFP foci within the nuclear volume, respectively (Materials and Methods). ii The proportions of these two categories (left Y axis) in WT (NKY4000) and ndj1Delta, csm4Delta, and ndj1Delta csm4Delta backgrounds (NKY3906, NKY3904, and NKY3905, respectively) were plotted as a function of time in sporulation media (SPM), with full and empty symbols on black lines corresponding to peripheral and dispersed proportions, respectively (Note: the differences between the two categories, while not absolute, is sufficiently robust that an untrained observer can place nearly all nuclei (>90%) in one category or the other without ambiguity. This robustness is further supported by the regular progression of changes in disposition over time not only in WT but also in mutants (below). The right Y axis and gray lines indicate the percentage of cells that have completed at least MI. B) Analysis of "bouquet" distribution in fixed nuclei. i 2D projections from 3D acquisition of Rap1-GFP signals, with the position of the SPB indicated by a blue circle (Materials and Methods). The nuclei presented here display peripherally localized telomere signals with either no evidence of bouquet colocalization or with "loose" and "tight" colocalization in the vicinity of the SPB. The region where most Rap1 foci co-localize is framed by a pink dotted rectangle. White dotted line indicates the outline of the nucleus, as estimated from Rap1-GFP background. Complete sets of 3D Rap1-GFP images and definitions of the three categories are provided in Figure S1. ii Proportion over time of nuclei exhibiting a "bouquet" configuration in WT, ndj1Delta, csm4Delta, and ndj1Delta csm4Delta backgrounds (NKY4000, NKY3906, NKY3904, and NKY3905, respectively; approximately100 nuclei per timepoint). C) Chromosome motion during prophase. i For statistical convenience, the histograms of the x and y step-sizes of Rap1-GFP foci, recorded every second over 1 min, were plotted prior to zygotene (t = 2 h for WT and ndj1Delta/csm4Delta mutants, respectively), and during zygotene stages (t = 4, 5, 6, and 5 h for WT, ndj1Delta, csm4Delta, and ndj1Delta csm4Delta backgrounds, respectively; Materials and Methods). Red curves indicate the distribution expected when assuming that the displacements follow a Normal distribution. For WT t = 4 h, the blue curve represent the expected Normal distribution of the step sizes included within v (t = 2 h)+/-2 S.D. (i.e. 77% of total measurements). v indicates the mean velocity of telomeric 2D displacements over time. An accompanying asterisk indicates a statistically significant difference in velocity the between pre-zygotene and zygotene stages (t-test, 0.05 significance level; Material and Methods). D) Csm4 localization in WT cells expressing Rap1-RFP and GFP-Csm4 (EAY1797). Fixed cells were spread and hybridized with anti-GFP antibodies. Pink and turquoise lines outline the nuclear peripheral Csm4 signal and co-localization of Rap1 and Csm4 signals, respectively. All scale bars represent 2 microm.
###end p 30
###begin p 31
###xml 72 92 72 92 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Material and Methods</xref>
###xml 396 399 396 399 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Koszul1">[6]</xref>
Cells were taken through synchronous meiosis under standard conditions (Material and Methods). In such cultures, at any given time point, the majority of nuclei are in one particular stage. Specifically, at t = 2, 3, 4 and 5 h, the majority of nuclei are in G2, leptotene, zygotene, and pachytene respectively, as defined by fluorescence activated cell sorter (FACS) analysis and SC status (e.g. [6] and below). Zygotene and pachytene nuclei can thus be defined operationally by the population average behavior of nuclei at t = 4 and t = 5 h, respectively, albeit with some "contamination" from other stages at each time point.
###end p 31
###begin title 32
WT Meiosis
###end title 32
###begin p 33
###xml 156 165 156 165 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2A</xref>
###xml 243 263 243 263 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Material and Methods</xref>
###xml 369 378 369 378 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2A</xref>
###xml 767 771 743 747 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken2">[15]</xref>
###xml 873 882 849 858 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2A</xref>
###xml 1194 1198 1170 1174 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Bass1">[30]</xref>
###xml 1199 1203 1175 1179 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Zickler2">[31]</xref>
###xml 1220 1225 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Organization of Rap1-GFP foci was analyzed in nuclei of living cells by 3D acquisition, in which a series of 400 nm optical z-sections are taken over time (Figure 2A; n = 50 cells at every time point; 10 planes total, exposure time of 900 ms; Material and Methods). In WT mitotic cells (t = 0 h in SPM), nuclei could be sorted by visual inspection into two categories (Figure 2A, panel i, panel ii, t = 0): approximately60% showed a ring of Rap1-GFP foci located in the periphery of the main chromosomal mass, with no clearly discernable internal foci, implying that telomeres are located "peripherally". The remaining approximately40% clearly showed internal foci, implying a "dispersed" disposition pattern. Similar categories have been seen in other studies (e.g. [15]). In contrast, by 2 h after initiation of meiosis, most nuclei were in the peripheral configuration (Figure 2A, panel ii). This progression presumably reflects complete migration of telomeres to the nuclear periphery via formation of meiosis-specific telomere/NE complexes that have assembled in early prophase. Since meiotic telomere/NE association at G2/leptotene is a regular feature of meiosis in many organisms (e.g. [30],[31]), we infer that yeast exhibits this same progression but with a prior "background" from mitotic telomere/NE association.
###end p 33
###begin p 34
###xml 445 449 445 449 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Sage1">[32]</xref>
###xml 1003 1012 997 1006 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2C</xref>
###xml 1059 1080 1053 1074 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Materials and Methods</xref>
###xml 1296 1299 1284 1287 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Koszul1">[6]</xref>
###xml 1427 1430 1409 1412 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken1">[5]</xref>
###xml 1431 1434 1413 1416 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Koszul1">[6]</xref>
###xml 1495 1504 1477 1486 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2C</xref>
###xml 1659 1668 1641 1650 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2C</xref>
###xml 2239 2242 2165 2168 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Scherthan1">[2]</xref>
###xml 2243 2246 2169 2172 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Koszul1">[6]</xref>
###xml 2247 2251 2173 2177 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad3">[24]</xref>
Living cells were also analyzed for the movement of Rap1-GFP foci. For this purpose, the focal plane of the microscope was set at the top of each examined nucleus so that movements around the nuclear periphery could be observed in apparent two dimensions. Frames were taken at one-second intervals over a period of one minute. The positions of the spots present in such focal planes were recorded and analyzed using SpotTracker2D ImageJ plug-in [32], when the amplitude of the displacement was limited, or manually at t = 4 h (below). Such analysis was performed for 5-12 Rap1 foci taken from a minimum of 5 different nuclei (yielding a total of 340 one-second step-sizes for both time points). At t = 2 h, when telomeres have reached their peripheral localization, the average velocity of movement (v) through two-dimensional space was 0.07+/-0.05 (S.D.) microm/sec. Further, these step sizes exhibit nearly (but not perfectly) a Gaussian distribution, suggesting that all foci are behaving similarly (Figure 2C, red curve; for details see figure legend and Materials and Methods). The same features are also seen previously at t = 0 h (average velocity 0.06+/-0.05 microm/sec with a near-Gaussian step-size distribution), in accord with the fact that active motion has not yet begun by t = 2 h [6]. In contrast, at t = 4 h, foci exhibit an increased average velocity of 0.23+/-0.23 microm/sec, in accord with earlier studies [5],[6]. Moreover step-sizes no longer fit a Gaussian distribution (Figure 2C, red curve); instead, there appear to be two types of movement, with a majority of steps being smaller and corresponding to a near-Gaussian distribution (Figure 2C, blue curve; approximately77% of total) plus a minority of much larger steps. The two apparent subpopulations exhibit velocities of approximately0.1 and approximately0.45 microm/sec, respectively, both of which are greater than the velocity observed at t = 2 h (approximately0.07 microm/sec). The existence of two such populations is in good agreement with the fact that, during the period of active actin-mediated movement, only a subset of telomeres are directly coupled to the motion-generating mechanism while others are either unaffected or dragged along passively [2],[6],[24].
###end p 34
###begin p 35
###xml 168 189 168 189 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Materials and Methods</xref>
###xml 522 531 522 531 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2B</xref>
###xml 545 554 545 554 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pgen.1000188.s001">Figure S1</xref>
###xml 683 692 683 692 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2B</xref>
###xml 784 792 784 792 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">Sordaria</italic>
###xml 830 833 830 833 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken1">[5]</xref>
###xml 834 838 834 838 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Harper1">[33]</xref>
###xml 874 878 874 878 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Kosaka1">[23]</xref>
###xml 744 749 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
To determine the overall disposition of telomeres at various stages, formaldehyde-fixed nuclei were analyzed in 3D by collection of an appropriate set of "z-sections" (Materials and Methods; n = 100 nuclei for each time point). Nuclei in which bright Rap1-GFP foci (i.e. the telomeres) were in a peripheral configuration (above) were scored with respect to whether most of the signals were or were not detectably clustered and, if so, whether those clusters occurred in the vicinity of the SPB (representative examples in Figure 2Bi; details in Figure S1). SPB-associated colocalization was defined as "bouquet". Such configurations include both "loose bouquet" and "tight bouquet" (Figure 2B, panel i), a distinction previously documented for yeast and several other organisms (e.g. Sordaria, D. Zickler, personal communication; [5],[33], Kosaka et al., accompanying paper [23]).
###end p 35
###begin p 36
###xml 264 273 264 273 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2B</xref>
###xml 402 405 402 405 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken1">[5]</xref>
###xml 406 410 406 410 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad2">[13]</xref>
###xml 411 415 411 415 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken2">[15]</xref>
###xml 416 420 416 420 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad3">[24]</xref>
###xml 769 772 757 760 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken1">[5]</xref>
Bouquet nuclei gradually increased in frequency from 2 h after meiosis induction, peaked at t = 4-5 h (i.e. zygotene/pachytene), and then diminished dramatically when cells entered pachytene in accord with expected loss of the bouquet configuration at this stage (Figure 2B, panel ii; analogous results obtained in a second independent experiment, not shown). As also noted in early studies (e.g. Ref. [5],[13],[15],[24]), the proportion of bouquet nuclei is low (approximately20%) even at the peak time points. This likely reflects the fact that zygotene nuclei have the potential for telomeres to be in the bouquet configuration but are undergoing such complex dynamic telomere movements that all telomeres are only present in a common area some fraction of the time [5].
###end p 36
###begin title 37
###xml 0 19 0 11 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;/csm4&#916;</italic>
ndj1Delta/csm4Delta Mutant Meiosis
###end title 37
###begin p 38
###xml 0 19 0 11 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;/csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 183 192 175 180 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 285 289 273 277 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken2">[15]</xref>
###xml 311 320 299 304 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 365 374 349 358 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2A</xref>
###xml 505 509 489 493 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Kosaka1">[23]</xref>
###xml 524 543 508 519 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916; csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 571 580 547 552 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 592 601 564 573 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2A</xref>
###xml 732 736 704 708 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken2">[15]</xref>
ndj1Delta/csm4Delta mutants and WT were analyzed for telomere-related events in parallel. All three mutants exhibit a WT mitotic-like configuration at t = 0. However, during meiosis, ndj1Delta telomeres fail to progress to a fully peripheral localization pattern (as shown previously; [15]) while, in contrast, csm4Delta telomeres behave indistinguishably from WT (Figure 2A, panel ii). Thus, while Ndj1 is required for meiosis-specific telomere/NE association, Csm4 is not, as also shown by Kosaka et al [23]. Further, the ndj1Delta csm4Delta double mutant exhibits the ndj1Delta phenotype (Figure 2A, panel ii), in accord with previous indications that Ndj1 localizes to telomeres and directly mediates their meiotic NE targeting [15].
###end p 38
###begin p 39
###xml 0 19 0 11 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;/csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 312 333 304 325 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Materials and Methods</xref>
###xml 590 593 582 585 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Scherthan1">[2]</xref>
###xml 625 634 617 626 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2C</xref>
###xml 645 648 637 640 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Koszul1">[6]</xref>
###xml 649 653 641 645 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Conrad3">[24]</xref>
###xml 822 828 814 820 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">per se</italic>
###xml 1000 1009 992 1001 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2C</xref>
###xml 1100 1109 1092 1097 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 1136 1145 1124 1129 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 1183 1193 1167 1177 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s3">Discussion</xref>
###xml 1363 1366 1347 1350 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Koszul1">[6]</xref>
###xml 1423 1432 1407 1412 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
ndj1Delta/csm4Delta mutants were analyzed for telomere movement at t = 0 and at zygotene, the time of which was defined for all three mutants by analysis of SC formation (below). Differences among different situations were evaluated for significance by comparison of step-size distributions by parametric tests (Materials and Methods). By this criterion, the following patterns emerge: (i) At t = 2 h, all three mutants exhibit velocities of movement similar to that seen in WT. (ii) At zygotene, all three mutants exhibit significantly less movement than WT, implying that Csm4, like Ndj1 [2] is required for active motion (Figure 2C; see also [6],[24]. Since telomeres are still NE-associated in the absence of Csm4, these findings suggest that this molecule is involved in the motion-producing force-generating process per se. (iii) Interestingly, from t = 0 to zygotene, there is a small but significant increase in motion in the absence of Ndj1 but no significant change in the absence of Csm4 (Figure 2C). There is also no significant increase when both proteins are absent. This suggests that csm4Delta is partially epistatic to ndj1Delta with respect to zygotene motion (see Discussion). Other studies further show that Ndj1 is not required for the NE deformations that signal actin-mediated motion while absence of Csm4 completely abrogates such motions [6]. Thus, the residual Csm4-dependent movement observed in ndj1Delta appears to reflect residual movement that is independent of meiosis-specific telomere/NE association, e.g. via mitotic-like or non-specific associations. In the absence of Csm4, in contrast, telomeres may simply be "not moving" or may actually be "locked in place."
###end p 39
###begin p 40
###xml 125 133 125 129 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj&#916;</italic>
###xml 135 144 131 136 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 153 172 145 156 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916; csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 188 197 172 181 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2B</xref>
###xml 257 266 241 246 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
###xml 266 270 246 250 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-TrellesSticken2">[15]</xref>
###xml 279 288 259 264 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 306 310 282 286 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Kosaka1">[23]</xref>
In accord with abrogation of telomere/NE association and/or chromosome movement, there is no detectable bouquet formation in ndjDelta, csm4Delta, or the ndj1Delta csm4Delta double mutant (Figure 2B, panel ii). This is also consistent with data reported for ndj1Delta[15] and for csm4Delta by Kosaka et al. [23].
###end p 40
###begin title 41
Csm4 Co-Localizes with Telomeric Rap1-GFP at the Nuclear Periphery
###end title 41
###begin p 42
###xml 201 205 201 205 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">CSM4</italic>
###xml 240 261 240 261 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="sec" rid="s4">Materials and Methods</xref>
###xml 543 552 543 552 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2D</xref>
###xml 616 625 616 625 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g002">Figure 2D</xref>
We also explored the cytological localization of Csm4 during meiosis in relation to the localization of telomeres using a strain (EAY1797) carrying an integrated Csm4-GFP fusion driven from the native CSM4 promoter and the Rap1-RFP fusion (Materials and Methods). Intrinsic Csm4-GFP fluorescence is sufficiently weak so that localization can only be assessed with anti-GFP antibody in fixed cells, and even then, with substantial background staining. Nonetheless, at mid-prophase, Csm4 can be seen in foci around the periphery of the nucleus (Figure 2D, left). These foci often overlap with strong foci of Rap1-RFP (Figure 2D, right). These images provide evidence suggestive of NE localization of Csm4 and a tendency for association with telomeres. A strain expressing only Rap1-RFP does not show such patterns (data not shown; strain NKY4005).
###end p 42
###begin title 43
Genetic Analysis of Recombination
###end title 43
###begin p 44
###xml 226 235 226 231 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
Defects in MI homolog segregation often reflect defects in the formation of crossovers (COs). Further, it would be interesting to know whether/how telomere dynamics affect recombination. We therefore examined recombination in csm4Delta by both genetic (this section) and physical analyses (below).
###end p 44
###begin title 45
###xml 27 36 27 32 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
Increased Crossing Over in csm4Delta
###end title 45
###begin p 46
###xml 81 90 81 86 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 92 100 88 96 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g003">Figure 3</xref>
###xml 102 110 98 106 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pgen-1000188-t001">Tables 1</xref>
###xml 112 114 108 110 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pgen.1000188.s005">S1</xref>
###xml 120 122 116 118 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pgen.1000188.s006">S2</xref>
###xml 129 138 125 130 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 289 298 281 290 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3-LEU2</italic>
###xml 303 312 295 304 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ADE2-HIS3</italic>
###xml 423 427 415 419 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Martini1">[34]</xref>
###xml 428 432 420 424 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="bibr" rid="pgen.1000188-Hoffmann1">[35]</xref>
###xml 442 451 434 443 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">LEU2-LYS2</italic>
###xml 471 480 463 472 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">LYS2-ADE2</italic>
###xml 503 512 495 504 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g003">Figure 3A</xref>
###xml 556 565 548 557 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">LEU2-LYS2</italic>
###xml 637 646 629 638 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g003">Figure 3A</xref>
###xml 718 727 710 715 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 899 908 887 896 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HIS4-LEU2</italic>
###xml 985 994 973 978 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 1014 1023 998 1007 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="fig" rid="pgen-1000188-g003">Figure 3B</xref>
We examined crossing over in 12 different intervals by tetrad analysis in WT and csm4Delta (Figure 3, Tables 1, S1, and S2). The csm4Delta mutation conferred a 30-40% increase in the level of COs for all four intervals in the SK1 congenic strains. In the analysis of complete tetrads, the URA3-LEU2 and ADE2-HIS3 intervals were significantly different from WT (G-test, p<0.007, 95% confidence level, Dunn-Sidak correction, [34],[35]) but the LEU2-LYS2 (p = 0.07) and the LYS2-ADE2 (p = 0.013) were not (Figure 3A). However, in the spore analysis, only the LEU2-LYS2 interval (p = 0.014) was not significantly different from WT (p<0.007, Figure 3A). Similarly, in isogenic SK1 strains, CO frequencies were increased in csm4Delta mutants at four out of eight analyzed intervals in complete tetrads and at six out of eight intervals in the spore analysis (G-test, p<0.05, 95% confidence level). At the HIS4-LEU2 interval on chromosome III, CO levels were indistinguishable between WT and csm4Delta in both data sets (Figure 3B).
###end p 46
###begin title 47
###xml 40 49 40 45 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">csm4&#916;</italic>
###xml 51 60 47 52 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">mlh1&#916;</italic>
###xml 62 71 54 59 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">msh5&#916;</italic>
###xml 77 86 65 70 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ndj1&#916;</italic>
Cumulative genetic distance (cM) in WT, csm4Delta, mlh1Delta, msh5Delta, and ndj1Delta strains.
###end title 47
###begin p 48
###xml 167 171 167 171 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 176 180 176 180 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">HIS3</italic>
###xml 322 326 322 326 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">ADE2</italic>
###xml 331 335 331 335 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">LEU2</italic>
###xml 355 359 355 359 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">URA3</italic>
###xml 364 368 364 368 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">CUP1</italic>
###xml 393 397 393 397 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">LYS5</italic>
###xml 402 406 402 406 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">TRP5</italic>
###xml 513 521 513 521 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="table" rid="pgen-1000188-t001">Tables 1</xref>
###xml 526 528 526 528 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" ref-type="supplementary-material" rid="pgen.1000188.s006">S2</xref>
In panels A and B, each bar is divided in sectors corresponding to genetic intervals in the region of the chromosome analyzed. A) Cumulative genetic distances between URA3 and HIS3 on chromosome XV in EAY1108/EAY1112 derived strains measured from tetrads (T) and single spores (S).
